<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Noah's Ark</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">Ark of the Covenant</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark_(disambiguation)" title="Noah's Ark (disambiguation)">Noah's Ark (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noahs_Ark.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="942" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Noahs_Ark.jpg/220px-Noahs_Ark.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Noahs_Ark.jpg/330px-Noahs_Ark.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Noahs_Ark.jpg/440px-Noahs_Ark.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noahs_Ark.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Noah's Ark</i> (1846), a painting by the American folk painter <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hicks" title="Edward Hicks">Edward Hicks</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Noah's Ark</b> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">תיבת נח</span>‎‎; <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>: <i>Tevat Noaḥ</i>) is the vessel in the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_flood_narrative" title="Genesis flood narrative">Genesis flood narrative</a> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> chapters 6–9) by which <a href="/wiki/God_in_Abrahamic_religions" title="God in Abrahamic religions">God</a> spares <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>, his family, and a remnant of all the world's animals from a world-engulfing flood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> According to Genesis, God gave Noah instructions for building the ark. Seven days before the deluge, God told Noah to enter the ark with his household and the animals. The story goes on to describe the ark being afloat for 150 days and then coming to rest on the <a href="/wiki/Mountains_of_Ararat" title="Mountains of Ararat">Mountains of Ararat</a> and the subsequent receding of the waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The story is repeated, with variations, in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, where the ark appears as <i>Safina <a href="/wiki/Noah_in_Islam" title="Noah in Islam">Nūḥ</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">سفينة نوح</span>‎‎ "Noah's boat"). The Genesis flood narrative is similar to numerous other <a href="/wiki/Flood_myth" title="Flood myth">flood myths</a> from a variety of cultures. The earliest known written flood myth is the <a href="/wiki/Ziusudra#Sumerian_flood_myth" title="Ziusudra">Sumerian flood myth</a> found in the <i>Epic of Ziusudra</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBandstra200861.2C_62_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBandstra200861.2C_62-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Searches_for_Noah%27s_Ark" title="Searches for Noah's Ark">Searches for Noah's Ark</a> have been made from at least the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius</a> (c. 275–339 CE) to the present day. There is no scientific evidence for a global flood, and despite many expeditions, no evidence of the ark has been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanTurk_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlanTurk-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noah.27s_Ark_Quest_Dead_in_Water_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noah.27s_Ark_Quest_Dead_in_Water-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_University_Press_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_University_Press-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline_2009_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cline_2009-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feder_2010_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feder_2010-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaak1998_Young1995_Isaak2006_Morton2001_Isaak2007173Stewart2010123Schadewald198212.E2.80.9317Scott2003_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaak1998_Young1995_Isaak2006_Morton2001_Isaak2007173Stewart2010123Schadewald198212.E2.80.9317Scott2003-10">[10]</a></sup> The challenges associated with housing all living animal types, and even plants, would have made building the ark a practical impossibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1983_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore1983-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span id="Ark:_Genesis_6–9"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ark:_Genesis_6.E2.80.939">Ark: Genesis 6–9</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ark: Genesis 6–9">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Hebrew word for the ark, <i>teba</i>, occurs twice in the Bible, in the flood narrative and in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a>, where it refers to the basket in which <a href="/wiki/Jochebed" title="Jochebed">Jochebed</a> places the infant <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>. (The word for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_covenant" title="Ark of the covenant">ark of the covenant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> is quite different.) In both cases <i>teba</i> has a connection with salvation from waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levenson_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levenson-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:21</sup></p>
<p>Noah is warned of the coming flood and told to construct the ark. God spells out to Noah the dimensions of the vessel: 300 <a href="/wiki/Cubit" title="Cubit">cubits</a> in length, 50 cubits in width and 30 cubits in height (450 × 75 × 45 ft or 137 × 22.9 × 13.7 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plaut_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-plaut-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It had three internal divisions (which are not actually called "decks", although presumably this is what is intended), a door in the side, and a <i>tsohar</i>, which may be either a roof or a skylight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton1990280.E2.80.93282_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton1990280.E2.80.93282-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is made of "<a href="/wiki/Gopher_wood" title="Gopher wood">gopher</a>" wood, a word which appears nowhere else in the Bible, and is divided into <i>qinnim</i>, a word which always refers to birds' nests elsewhere, leading some scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2017)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> to emend this to <i>qanim</i> (reeds), the material used for the boat of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrahasis" title="Atrahasis">Atrahasis</a>, the Babylonian flood-hero. God instructs Noah to <i>kapar</i> (smear) the ark with <i>koper</i> (<a href="/wiki/Pitch_(resin)" title="Pitch (resin)">pitch</a>): in Hebrew the first of these words is a verb formed from the second and, like "gopher", it is a word found nowhere else in the Bible.</p>
<p>Noah is instructed to take on board his wife, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sons_of_Noah" title="Sons of Noah">his three sons</a>, and his sons' wives. He is also to take two of every living thing, and seven pairs of every clean creature and of every bird, together with sufficient food.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology:_the_ark_as_microcosm">Theology: the ark as microcosm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Theology: the ark as microcosm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The story of the flood closely parallels the story of the creation: a cycle of creation, un-creation, and re-creation, in which the ark plays a pivotal role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGooder200538_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGooder200538-17">[17]</a></sup> The universe as conceived by the ancient Hebrews comprised a flat disk-shaped habitable earth with the heavens above and <a href="/wiki/Sheol" title="Sheol">Sheol</a>, the underworld of the dead, below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnight1990175.E2.80.93176_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnight1990175.E2.80.93176-18">[18]</a></sup> These three were surrounded by a watery "ocean" of chaos, protected by the <a href="/wiki/Firmament" title="Firmament">firmament</a>, a transparent but solid dome resting on the mountains which ringed the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnight1990175.E2.80.93176_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnight1990175.E2.80.93176-18">[18]</a></sup> Noah's three-deck ark represents this three-level Hebrew cosmos in miniature: the heavens, the earth, and the waters beneath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKesslerDeurloo200481_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKesslerDeurloo200481-19">[19]</a></sup> In Genesis 1, God created the three-level world as a space in the midst of the waters for humanity; in Genesis 6–8 (the flood narrative) he fills that space with waters again, saving only Noah, his family and the animals with him in the ark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGooder200538_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGooder200538-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition_of_the_flood_narrative">Composition of the flood narrative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Composition of the flood narrative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is a consensus among scholars that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentateuch" title="Pentateuch">Pentateuch</a> (the first five books of the Bible, beginning with Genesis) was the product of a long and complex process that was not completed until after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_exile" title="Babylonian exile">Babylonian exile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEnns201223_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnns201223-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Comparative_mythology:_the_Babylonian_origins_of_Noah's_ark"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_mythology:_the_Babylonian_origins_of_Noah.27s_ark">Comparative mythology: the Babylonian origins of Noah's ark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Comparative mythology: the Babylonian origins of Noah's ark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flood_myth" title="Flood myth">Flood myth</a></div>
<p>For well over a century scholars have recognised that the Bible's story of Noah's ark is based on older Mesopotamian models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKvanvig2011210_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKvanvig2011210-21">[21]</a></sup> Because all these flood stories deal with events that allegedly happened at the dawn of history, they give the impression that the myths themselves must come from very primitive origins. But in fact, the myth of the global flood that destroys all life only begins to appear in the <a href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_Dynasty" title="First Babylonian Dynasty">Old Babylonian period</a> (20th–16th centuries BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen20133-4_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen20133-4-22">[22]</a></sup> The reasons for this emergence of the typical Mesopotamian flood myth may have been bound up with the specific circumstances of the end of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="Third Dynasty of Ur">Third Dynasty of Ur</a> around 2004 BCE and the restoration of order by the First Dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2013253_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2013253-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are nine known versions of the Mesopotamian flood story, each more or less adapted from an earlier version. In the oldest version, the hero is King <a href="/wiki/Ziusudra" title="Ziusudra">Ziusudra</a> and this version was inscribed around 1600 BCE in the Sumerian city of <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>. It is known as the Sumerian Flood Story, and probably derives from an earlier version. The Ziusudra version tells how he builds a boat and rescues life, when the gods decide to destroy it. This remains the basic plot for several subsequent flood-stories and heroes, including Noah. Ziusudra's Sumerian name means "He of long life". In Babylonian versions his name is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrahasis" title="Atrahasis">Atrahasis</a>, but the meaning is the same. In the Atrahasis version, the flood is a river flood (lines 6–9 Atrahasis III,iv)</p>
<p>Probably the most famous version is contained in a longer work called the <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a>, now known only from a 1st millennium <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> copy in which the flood hero is named <a href="/wiki/Utnapishtim" title="Utnapishtim">Utnapishtim</a>, "He-found-life". (Gilgamesh is the hero of the complete epic, not the flood story hero).</p>
<p>The last known version of the Mesopotamian flood story was written in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> in the 3rd century BCE by a Babylonian priest named <a href="/wiki/Berossus" title="Berossus">Berossus</a>. From the fragments that survive, it seems little changed from the versions of two thousand years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkel201489-101_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkel201489-101-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The version closest to the Biblical story of Noah, as well as its most likely source, is that of Utnapishtim in the Epic of Gilgamesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigosian200440_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigosian200440-25">[25]</a></sup> The most complete text of Utnapishtim's story is a clay tablet dating from the 7th century BCE, but fragments of the story have been found from as far back as the 19th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigosian200440_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigosian200440-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The parallels – both similarities and differences – between Noah's Ark and the boat of the Babylonian flood-hero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrahasis" title="Atrahasis">Atrahasis</a> have often been noted. Noah's ark is rectangular, while Atrahasis was instructed to build his in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Cube" title="Cube">cube</a>; Atrahasis's ark has seven decks with nine compartments on each level, while Noah's has three decks, but he is not given any instructions on the number of compartments to build.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton1990282_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton1990282-26">[26]</a></sup> The word used for "pitch" (sealing tar or resin) is not the normal Hebrew word, but is closely related to the word used in the Babylonian story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKeown200855_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKeown200855-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The causes for God/gods having sent the flood also differ. In the Hebrew narrative the flood comes as God's judgment on a wicked humanity. In the Babylonian <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a> the reasons are not given and the flood appears to be the result of the caprice of the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May_Metzger_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-May_Metzger-28">[28]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrahasis" title="Atrahasis">Atrahasis</a> version of the Babylonian flood story, the flood was sent by the gods to reduce human over-population, and after the flood, other measures were introduced to prevent the problem recurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rabbinic_Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rabbinic Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2273" data-file-width="1576" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg/200px-Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg/300px-Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg/400px-Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franz%C3%B6sischer_Meister_um_1675_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Building of Noah's Ark (painting by a French master of 1675).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Noah_in_rabbinic_literature" title="Noah in rabbinic literature">Noah in rabbinic literature</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmudic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">tractates</a> <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin_(tractate)" title="Sanhedrin (tractate)">Sanhedrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avodah_Zarah" title="Avodah Zarah">Avodah Zarah</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zevahim" title="Zevahim">Zevahim</a> relate that, while Noah was building the ark, he attempted to warn his neighbors of the coming deluge, but was ignored or mocked. In order to protect Noah and his family, God placed lions and other ferocious animals to guard them from the wicked who tried to stop them from entering the ark. According to one <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a>, it was God, or the <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a>, who gathered the animals to the ark, together with their food. As there had been no need to distinguish between clean and unclean animals before this time, the clean animals made themselves known by kneeling before Noah as they entered the ark. A differing opinion said that the ark itself distinguished clean animals from unclean, admitting seven pairs each of the former and one pair each of the latter.</p>
<p>According to Sanhedrin 108b, Noah was engaged both day and night in feeding and caring for the animals, and did not sleep for the entire year aboard the ark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The animals were the best of their species, and so behaved with utmost goodness. They abstained from procreation, so that the number of creatures that disembarked was exactly equal to the number that embarked. The raven created problems, refusing to leave the ark when Noah sent it forth and accusing the patriarch of wishing to destroy its race, but as the commentators pointed out, God wished to save the raven, for its descendants were destined to feed the prophet <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>.</p>
<p>According to one tradition, refuse was stored on the lowest of the ark's three decks, humans and clean beasts on the second, and the unclean animals and birds on the top; a differing interpretation described the refuse as being stored on the utmost deck, from where it was shoveled into the sea through a trapdoor. Precious stones, said to be as bright as the noon sun, provided light, and God ensured that food remained fresh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE_Noah_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-JE_Noah-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ark_of_Noah_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ark_of_Noah-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Some more unorthodox interpretations of the ark narrative also surfaced: the 12th-century Jewish commentator <a href="/wiki/Abraham_ibn_Ezra" title="Abraham ibn Ezra">Abraham ibn Ezra</a> interpreted the ark as being a vessel that remained underwater for 40 days, after which it floated to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="862" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png/200px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png/300px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png/400px-Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_chronicles_f_11r_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An artist's depiction of the construction of the Ark, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Nuremberg Chronicle</a></i> (1493).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="1536" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg/220px-Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg/330px-Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg/440px-Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woodcut_of_Noah%27s_Ark_from_Anton_Koberger%27s_%22German_Bible%22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A woodcut of Noah's Ark from <a href="/wiki/Anton_Koberger" title="Anton Koberger">Anton Koberger</a>'s German Bible</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Interpretations of the ark narrative played an important role in early Christian doctrine. The <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Peter" title="First Epistle of Peter">First Epistle of Peter</a> (composed around the end of the first century AD<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>) compared Noah's salvation through water to salvation through water in baptism.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Pt+3%3A20%E2%80%9321&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[1Pt 3:20–21]</a></sup></p>
<p>St. <a href="/wiki/Hippolytus_of_Rome" title="Hippolytus of Rome">Hippolytus of Rome</a> (died 235) sought to demonstrate that "the Ark was a symbol of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a> who was expected", stating that the vessel had its door on the east side – the direction from which Christ would appear at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> – and that the bones of <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a> were brought aboard, together with gold, <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Myrrh" title="Myrrh">myrrh</a> (the symbols of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Christ" title="Nativity of Christ">Nativity of Christ</a>). Hippolytus furthermore stated that the ark floated to and fro in the four directions on the waters, making the sign of the cross, before eventually landing on Mount Kardu "in the east, in the land of the sons of Raban, and the Orientals call it Mount Godash; the <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> call it Ararat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knight.2C_K_2007_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knight.2C_K_2007-38">[38]</a></sup> On a more practical plane, Hippolytus explained that the lowest of the three decks was for wild beasts, the middle for birds and domestic animals, and the top level for humans. He says that male animals were separated from the females by sharp stakes so that there would be no breeding on board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knight.2C_K_2007_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Knight.2C_K_2007-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Father" title="Church Father">Church Father</a> and theologian <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> (c. 182–251), in response to a critic who doubted that the ark could contain all the animals in the world, argued that Moses, the traditional author of the book of Genesis, had been brought up in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> and would therefore have used the larger Egyptian cubit. He also fixed the shape of the ark as a truncated <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a>, square at its base, and tapering to a square peak one cubit on a side; it was not until the 12th century that it came to be thought of as a rectangular box with a sloping roof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohn199938_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohn199938-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early Christian artists depicted Noah standing in a small box on the waves, symbolizing God saving the Christian Church in its turbulent early years. St. <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> (354–430), in his work <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_God_(book)" title="City of God (book)">City of God</a></i>, demonstrated that the dimensions of the ark corresponded to the dimensions of the human body, which according to Christian doctrine is the body of Christ and in turn the body of the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaff.2C_P_1890_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaff.2C_P_1890-40">[40]</a></sup> St. <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> (c. 347–420) identified the raven, which was sent forth and did not return, as the "foul bird of wickedness" expelled by <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> more enduringly, the dove and olive branch came to symbolize the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> and the hope of <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> and eventually, peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohn_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn-42">[42]</a></sup> The olive branch remains a secular and religious <a href="/wiki/Peace_symbols" title="Peace symbols">symbol of peace</a> today.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ussher_chronology" title="Ussher chronology">Ussher's chronology</a>, one of the most prominent attempts to date events according to the Bible, calculated that Noah would have lived from 2948 until 1998 BCE, with the deluge occurring in 2349 BCE. Calculations based on figures in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> place the flood in 1656 AM (<i><a href="/wiki/Anno_Mundi" title="Anno Mundi">Anno Mundi</a></i>); those based on the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LXX" title="LXX">LXX</a> Bible in 2262 AM; and those based on the <a href="/wiki/Samaritan_Pentateuch" title="Samaritan Pentateuch">Samaritan Pentateuch</a>, in 1308 AM. The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jubilees" title="Book of Jubilees">Book of Jubilees</a>, by a different calculation, also yields the date 1308 AM for the flood.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Noah_in_Islam" title="Noah in Islam">Noah in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3538" data-file-width="2756" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg/220px-16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg/330px-16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg/440px-16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:16_2-8-2005-Noahs-ark-Hafis-Abru-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Miniature from Hafiz-i Abru's Majma al-tawarikh. <i>Noah's Ark</i> Iran (Afghanistan), Herat; Timur's son Shah Rukh (1405–1447) ordered the historian <a href="/wiki/Hafiz-i_Abru" title="Hafiz-i Abru">Hafiz-i Abru</a> to write a continuation of <a href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid al-Din's</a> famous history of the world, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jami_al-tawarikh" title="Jami al-tawarikh">Jami al-tawarikh</a>. Like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Il-Khanids" title="Il-Khanids">Il-Khanids</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timurids" title="Timurids">Timurids</a> were concerned with legitimizing their right to rule, and Hafiz-i Abru's <i>A Collection of Histories</i> covers a period that included the time of <a href="/wiki/Shahrukh_Mirza" title="Shahrukh Mirza">Shah Rukh</a> himself.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noah%27s_ark_and_the_deluge.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="266" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Noah%27s_ark_and_the_deluge.JPG/220px-Noah%27s_ark_and_the_deluge.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Noah%27s_ark_and_the_deluge.JPG 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noah%27s_ark_and_the_deluge.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Noah's ark and the deluge from Zubdat-al Tawarikh</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In contrast to the Jewish tradition, which uses a term that can be translated as a "box" or "<a href="/wiki/Chest_(furniture)" title="Chest (furniture)">chest</a>" to describe the Ark, surah 29:15 of the Quran refers to it as a <i>safina</i>, an ordinary ship, and surah 54:13 describes the ark as "a thing of boards and nails". <a href="/wiki/Abd_Allah_ibn_Abbas" title="Abd Allah ibn Abbas">Abd Allah ibn Abbas</a>, a contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, wrote that Noah was in doubt as to what shape to make the ark, and that Allah revealed to him that it was to be shaped like a bird's belly and fashioned of <a href="/wiki/Teak" title="Teak">teak</a> wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baidawi" title="Baidawi">Abdallah ibn 'Umar al-Baidawi</a>, writing in the 13th century, explains that in the first of its three levels wild and domesticated animals were lodged, in the second the human beings, and in the third the birds. On every plank was the name of a prophet. Three missing planks, symbolizing three prophets, were brought from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> by Og, son of Anak, the only one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" title="Giant (mythology)">giants</a> permitted to survive the Flood. The body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adam_(Bible)" title="Adam (Bible)">Adam</a> was carried in the middle to divide the men from the women. Surah 11:41 says: "And he said, 'Ride ye in it; in the Name of Allah it moves and stays!'"; this was taken to mean that Noah said, "In the Name of Allah!" when he wished the ark to move, and the same when he wished it to stand still.</p>
<p>Noah spent five or six months aboard the ark, at the end of which he sent out a raven. But the raven stopped to feast on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>, and so Noah cursed it and sent out the dove, which has been known ever since as the friend of humanity. The medieval scholar <a href="/wiki/Al-Masudi" title="Al-Masudi">Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Husayn Masudi</a> (died 956) wrote that Allah commanded the Earth to absorb the water, and certain portions which were slow in obeying received <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">salt water</a> in punishment and so became <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">dry and arid</a>. The water which was not absorbed formed the seas, so that the waters of the flood still exist. Masudi says that the ark began its voyage at <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a> in central <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and sailed to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, circling the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> before finally traveling to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Judi" title="Mount Judi">Mount Judi</a>, which surah 11:44 states was its final resting place. This mountain is identified by tradition with a hill near the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazirat_ibn_Umar" title="Jazirat ibn Umar">Jazirat ibn Umar</a> on the east bank of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> in the province of <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> in northern Iraq, and Masudi says that the spot could be seen in his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE_Noah_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-JE_Noah-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ark_of_Noah_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ark_of_Noah-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1047" data-file-width="1400" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg/200px-Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg/300px-Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg/400px-Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cole_Thomas_The_Subsiding_of_the_Waters_of_the_Deluge_1829.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Subsiding of the Waters of the Deluge</i> (1829), a painting by the American painter <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cole" title="Thomas Cole">Thomas Cole</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span id="Bahá'í"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bah.C3.A1.27.C3.AD">Bahá'í</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bahá'í">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a> regards the Ark and the Flood as symbolic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In Bahá'í belief, only Noah's followers were spiritually alive, preserved in the "ark" of his teachings, as others were spiritually dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BahaiArk_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-BahaiArk-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The Bahá'í scripture <i><a href="/wiki/Kit%C3%A1b-i-%C3%8Dq%C3%A1n" title="Kitáb-i-Íqán">Kitáb-i-Íqán</a></i> endorses the Islamic belief that Noah had numerous companions on the ark, either 40 or 72, as well as his family, and that he taught for 950 (symbolic) years before the flood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Bahá'í Faith was founded in 19th century Persia, and it recognizes divine messengers from both the Abrahamic and the Indian traditions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historicity">Historicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Historicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The practical challenges associated with building an ark large enough to house all living animal types, and even plants, would have been very considerable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1983_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore1983-11">[11]</a></sup> Various editions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i> reflect the collapse of belief in the historicity of the ark in the face of advancing scientific knowledge. Its 1771 edition offered the following as scientific evidence for the ark's size and capacity: "...Buteo and <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_Kircher" title="Athanasius Kircher">Kircher</a> have proved geometrically, that, taking the common <a href="/wiki/Cubit" title="Cubit">cubit</a> as a foot and a half, the ark was abundantly sufficient for all the animals supposed to be lodged in it ... the number of species of animals will be found much less than is generally imagined, not amounting to a hundred species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrupeds" title="Quadrupeds">quadrupeds</a>". By the eighth edition (1853–60), the encyclopedia said of the Noah story, "The insuperable difficulties connected with the belief that all other existing species of animals were provided for in the ark are obviated by adopting the suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Stillingfleet" title="Edward Stillingfleet">Bishop Stillingfleet</a>, approved by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Poole" title="Matthew Poole">Matthew Poole</a> ... and others, that the Deluge did not extend beyond the region of the Earth then inhabited". By the ninth edition, in 1875, no attempt was made to reconcile the Noah story with scientific fact, and it was presented without comment. In the 1960 edition, the article on the ark stated that "Before the days of '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higher_criticism" title="Higher criticism">higher criticism</a>' and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">modern scientific views as to the origin of the species</a>, there was much discussion among the learned, and many ingenious and curious theories were advanced, as to the number of animals on the ark".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ark_eb_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ark_eb-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Ark's_geometrics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ark.27s_geometrics">Ark's geometrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ark's geometrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1187" data-file-width="1800" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg/220px-Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg/330px-Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg/440px-Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allessandro_Masnago_-_Cameo_with_Noah%27s_Ark_-_Walters_421447.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This engraving, made from carved sardonyx and gold, features a line of animals on the gangway to Noah's ark. It is based on a woodcut by the French illustrator Bernard Salomon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The Walters Art Museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> saw much speculation on the nature of the ark that might have seemed familiar to early theologians such as <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustine" title="Augustine">Augustine</a>. At the same time, however, a new class of scholarship arose, one which, while never questioning the literal truth of the ark story, began to speculate on the practical workings of Noah's vessel from within a purely naturalistic framework. In the 15th century, Alfonso Tostada gave a detailed account of the logistics of the ark, down to arrangements for the disposal of dung and the circulation of fresh air. The 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometer</a> Johannes Buteo calculated the ship's internal dimensions, allowing room for Noah's grinding mills and smokeless ovens, a model widely adopted by other commentators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohn_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohn-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Searches_for_Noah's_Ark"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Searches_for_Noah.27s_Ark">Searches for Noah's Ark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Searches for Noah's Ark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Searches_for_Noah%27s_Ark" title="Searches for Noah's Ark">Searches for Noah's Ark</a></div>
<p>Searches for Noah's Ark have been made from at least the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius</a> (c.275–339 CE) to the present day. Various locations for the ark have been suggested but have never been confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanTurk_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-PlanTurk-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noah.27s_Ark_Quest_Dead_in_Water_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Noah.27s_Ark_Quest_Dead_in_Water-6">[6]</a></sup> The practice is widely regarded as <a href="/wiki/Pseudoarchaeology" title="Pseudoarchaeology">pseudoarchaeology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_University_Press_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_University_Press-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cline_2009_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cline_2009-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feder_2010_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feder_2010-9">[9]</a></sup> Search sites have included <a href="/wiki/Durup%C4%B1nar_site" title="Durupınar site">Durupınar site</a>, a site on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Tend%C3%BCrek" title="Mount Tendürek">Mount Tendürek</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ararat" title="Mount Ararat">Mount Ararat</a>, but geological investigation of possible remains of the ark has only shown natural sedimentary formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood_geology">Flood geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Flood geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flood_geology" title="Flood geology">Flood geology</a></div>
<p>Flood geology is the religiously-inspired interpretation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_history" title="Geological history">geological history</a> of the Earth in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Flood_myth" title="Flood myth">global flood</a> described in <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis 6–9</a>. Similar views played a part in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_geology" title="History of geology">early development of the science of geology</a>, even after the <a href="/wiki/Ussher_chronology" title="Ussher chronology">biblical chronology</a> had been rejected by <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geologists</a> in favour of an ancient Earth. Flood geology is a <a href="/wiki/Creation_science" title="Creation science">creation science</a>, which is a part of <a href="/wiki/Young_Earth_creationism" title="Young Earth creationism">young Earth creationism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkinson200424.E2.80.9327Numbers200610_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkinson200424.E2.80.9327Numbers200610-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern geology and its sub-disciplines utilize the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to analyze the geology of the earth. Flood geology contradicts the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a> in geology and <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, as well as that in related disciplines such as chemistry, physics, astronomy, cosmology, biology, geophysics and stratigraphy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung1995_Isaak2006_Morton2001_Isaak2007173Stewart2010123_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung1995_Isaak2006_Morton2001_Isaak2007173Stewart2010123-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> There is an absence of evidence for any of the effects proposed by flood geologists, and their claims concerning phenomena such as fossil layering are not taken seriously by scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIsaak1998_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaak1998-56">[56]</a></sup> More generally, the key tenets of flood geology are refuted by scientific analysis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung1995_Isaak2006_Morton2001_Isaak2007173Stewart2010123_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung1995_Isaak2006_Morton2001_Isaak2007173Stewart2010123-53">[53]</a></sup> and it is considered to be <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_community" title="Scientific community">scientific community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchadewald198212.E2.80.9317Scott2003_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchadewald198212.E2.80.9317Scott2003-57">[57]</a></sup> Author JJ Dyken notes that established civilizations in Egypt and China were not impacted by claims of a global flood during the time of Noah's Ark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Divine_Default_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Divine_Default-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Noah%27s_Ark&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In the six hundredth year of Noah's life, in the second month, the seventeenth day of the month" the flood began (Genesis 7:11, KJV). "The water receded steadily from the earth. <i>At the end of the hundred and fifty days</i> the water had gone down, and on <i>the seventeenth day of the seventh month</i> the ark came to rest on the mountains of Ararat. The waters continued to recede until the tenth month, and on the first day of the tenth month the tops of the mountains became visible." Genesis 8:3-5; The 17th day of the 7th month - the 17 day of the 2nd month = 5 months @ 30 days / month = 150 days.</span></li>
